<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Species</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Basic unit of taxonomic classification, below genus</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Species_(disambiguation)" title="Species (disambiguation)">Species (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><div class="noresize"><a href="/wiki/File:Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg" title="File:Biological classification L Pengo vflip.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="230" decoding="async" height="385" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg/150px-Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg/225px-Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg/300px-Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_36d5ffaa5ff6869a" width="150"/></a><map name="ImageMap_36d5ffaa5ff6869a"><area alt="Life" coords="65,10,147,37" href="/wiki/Life" shape="rect" title="Life"/><area alt="Domain" coords="65,51,147,78" href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" shape="rect" title="Domain"/><area alt="Kingdom" coords="65,93,147,120" href="/wiki/Kingdom_(biology)" shape="rect" title="Kingdom"/><area alt="Phylum" coords="65,134,147,161" href="/wiki/Phylum" shape="rect" title="Phylum"/><area alt="Class" coords="65,175,147,202" href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" shape="rect" title="Class"/><area alt="Order" coords="65,217,147,244" href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" shape="rect" title="Order"/><area alt="Family" coords="65,258,147,285" href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" shape="rect" title="Family"/><area alt="Genus" coords="65,299,147,327" href="/wiki/Genus" shape="rect" title="Genus"/><area alt="Species" coords="65,341,147,368" href="/wiki/Species" shape="rect" title="Species"/></map></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biological_classification_L_Pengo_vflip.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The hierarchy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">biological classification</a>'s eight major <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">taxonomic ranks</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> contains one or more species. Intermediate minor rankings are not shown.</div></div></div>
<p>
In <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, a <b>species</b> is the basic unit of <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">classification</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_rank" title="Taxonomic rank">taxonomic rank</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organism</a>, as well as a unit of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>. A species is often defined as the largest group of <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> in which any two individuals of the appropriate <a href="/wiki/Sex" title="Sex">sexes</a> or mating types can <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">produce</a> <a href="/wiki/Fertility" title="Fertility">fertile</a> <a href="/wiki/Offspring" title="Offspring">offspring</a>, typically by <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexual reproduction</a>. Other ways of defining species include their <a href="/wiki/Karyotype" title="Karyotype">karyotype</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> sequence, <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a>, behaviour or <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niche</a>. In addition, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleontologist" title="Paleontologist">paleontologists</a> use the concept of the <a href="/wiki/Chronospecies" title="Chronospecies">chronospecies</a> since <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> reproduction cannot be examined.
</p><p>The most recent rigorous estimate for the total number of species of <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> is between 8 and 8.7 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLOS_2011_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-PLOS_2011-3">[3]</a></sup> However, only about 14% of these had been described by 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PLOS_2011_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-PLOS_2011-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>All species (except <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>) are given a <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">two-part name</a>, a "binomial". The first part of a binomial is the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> to which the species belongs. The second part is called the <a href="/wiki/Specific_name_(zoology)" title="Specific name (zoology)">specific name</a> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_name_(botany)" title="Specific name (botany)">specific epithet</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Botanical_nomenclature" title="Botanical nomenclature">botanical nomenclature</a>, also sometimes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoological_nomenclature" title="Zoological nomenclature">zoological nomenclature</a>). For example, <i><a href="/wiki/Boa_constrictor" title="Boa constrictor">Boa constrictor</a></i> is one of the species of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Boa_(genus)" title="Boa (genus)">Boa</a></i>, with <i>constrictor</i> being the species' epithet.
</p><p>While the definitions given above may seem adequate at first glance, when looked at more closely they represent problematic <a href="/wiki/Species_concept" title="Species concept">species concepts</a>. For example, the boundaries between closely related species become unclear with <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridisation</a>, in a <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">species complex</a> of hundreds of similar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microspecies" title="Microspecies">microspecies</a>, and in a <a href="/wiki/Ring_species" title="Ring species">ring species</a>. Also, among organisms that reproduce only <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexually</a>, the concept of a reproductive species breaks down, and each clone is potentially a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microspecies" title="Microspecies">microspecies</a>. Although none of these are entirely satisfactory definitions, and while the concept of species may not be a perfect model of life, it is still an incredibly useful tool to scientists and <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservationists</a> for studying life on Earth, regardless of the theoretical difficulties. If species were fixed and clearly distinct from one another, there would be no problem, but <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> processes cause species to change. This obliges <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomists</a> to decide, for example, when enough change has occurred to declare that a lineage should be divided into multiple <a href="/wiki/Chronospecies" title="Chronospecies">chronospecies</a>, or when populations have diverged to have enough distinct character states to be described as <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">cladistic</a> species.
</p><p>Species were seen from the time of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> until the 18th century as fixed categories that could be arranged in a hierarchy, the <a href="/wiki/Great_chain_of_being" title="Great chain of being">great chain of being</a>. In the 19th century, biologists grasped that species could evolve given sufficient time. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>'s 1859 book <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">On the Origin of Species</a></i> explained how <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">species could arise</a> by <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>. That understanding was <a href="/wiki/Modern_synthesis_(20th_century)" title="Modern synthesis (20th century)">greatly extended in the 20th century</a> through <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> and population <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>. Genetic variability arises from <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutations</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recombination_(biology)" title="Recombination (biology)">recombination</a>, while organisms themselves are mobile, leading to <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">geographical isolation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_drift" title="Genetic drift">genetic drift</a> with varying <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_pressure" title="Evolutionary pressure">selection pressures</a>. Genes can sometimes be exchanged between species by <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">horizontal gene transfer</a>; new species can arise rapidly through hybridisation and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyploid" title="Polyploid">polyploidy</a>; and species may <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">become extinct</a> for a variety of reasons. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viruses" title="Viruses">Viruses</a> are a special case, driven by a <a href="/wiki/Mutation%E2%80%93selection_balance" title="Mutation–selection balance">balance of mutation and selection</a>, and can be treated as <a href="/wiki/Viral_quasispecies" title="Viral quasispecies">quasispecies</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Biologists and taxonomists have made many attempts to define species, beginning from <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> and moving towards <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>. Early taxonomists such as Linnaeus had no option but to describe what they saw: this was later formalised as the typological or morphological species concept. <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Mayr" title="Ernst Mayr">Ernst Mayr</a> emphasised reproductive isolation, but this, like other species concepts, is hard or even impossible to test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zelnio_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zelnio-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Later biologists have tried to refine Mayr's definition with the recognition and cohesion concepts, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Many of the concepts are quite similar or overlap, so they are not easy to count: the biologist R. L. Mayden recorded about 24 concepts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Claridge1997_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Claridge1997-7">[7]</a></sup> and the philosopher of science <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkins" title="John Wilkins">John Wilkins</a> counted 26.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zelnio_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zelnio-4">[4]</a></sup> Wilkins further grouped the species concepts into seven basic kinds of concepts: (1) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agamospecies" title="Agamospecies">agamospecies</a> for asexual organisms (2) biospecies for reproductively isolated sexual organisms (3) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecospecies" title="Ecospecies">ecospecies</a> based on ecological niches (4) evolutionary species based on lineage (5) genetic species based on gene pool (6) morphospecies based on form or phenotype and (7) taxonomic species, a species as determined by a taxonomist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZachos201679_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZachos201679-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Typological_species"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Typological_or_morphological_species">Typological or morphological species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Typological or morphological species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eurasian_blue_tit_Lancashire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2604" data-file-width="3472" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Eurasian_blue_tit_Lancashire.jpg/220px-Eurasian_blue_tit_Lancashire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Eurasian_blue_tit_Lancashire.jpg/330px-Eurasian_blue_tit_Lancashire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Eurasian_blue_tit_Lancashire.jpg/440px-Eurasian_blue_tit_Lancashire.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eurasian_blue_tit_Lancashire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>All adult <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_blue_tit" title="Eurasian blue tit">Eurasian blue tits</a> share the same coloration, unmistakably identifying the <i>morphospecies</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A typological species is a group of organisms in which individuals conform to certain fixed properties (a type), so that even pre-literate people often recognise the same taxon as do modern taxonomists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gould_1980_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gould_1980-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maynard_Smith_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maynard_Smith-11">[11]</a></sup> The clusters of variations or phenotypes within specimens (such as longer or shorter tails) would differentiate the species. This method was used as a "classical" method of determining species, such as with Linnaeus, early in evolutionary theory. However, different phenotypes are not necessarily different species (e.g. a four-winged <i><a href="/wiki/Drosophila" title="Drosophila">Drosophila</a></i> born to a two-winged mother is not a different species). Species named in this manner are called <i>morphospecies</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Robert_R._Sokal" title="Robert R. Sokal">Robert R. Sokal</a>, Theodore J. Crovello and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Sneath" title="Peter Sneath">Peter Sneath</a> proposed a variation on the morphological species concept, a <a href="/wiki/Phenetics" title="Phenetics">phenetic</a> species, defined as a set of organisms with a similar <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a> to each other, but a different phenotype from other sets of organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> It differs from the morphological species concept in including a numerical measure of distance or similarity to cluster entities based on multivariate comparisons of a reasonably large number of phenotypic traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Recognition_species"></span><span class="anchor" id="Cohesion_species"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition_and_cohesion_species">Recognition and cohesion species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Recognition and cohesion species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A mate-recognition species is a group of sexually reproducing organisms that recognise one another as potential mates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Expanding on this to allow for post-mating isolation, a cohesion species is the most inclusive population of individuals having the potential for phenotypic cohesion through intrinsic cohesion mechanisms; no matter whether populations can hybridise successfully, they are still distinct cohesion species if the amount of hybridisation is insufficient to completely mix their respective <a href="/wiki/Gene_pool" title="Gene pool">gene pools</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallet_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mallet-18">[18]</a></sup> A further development of the recognition concept is provided by the biosemiotic concept of species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Genetic_similarity"></span><span class="anchor" id="Barcode_species"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_similarity_and_barcode_species">Genetic similarity and barcode species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Genetic similarity and barcode species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_taxonomy#Species_concept" title="Bacterial taxonomy">Bacterial taxonomy § Species concept</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cytochrome_C_Oxidase_1OCC_in_Membrane_2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1122" data-file-width="1620" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Cytochrome_C_Oxidase_1OCC_in_Membrane_2.png/220px-Cytochrome_C_Oxidase_1OCC_in_Membrane_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Cytochrome_C_Oxidase_1OCC_in_Membrane_2.png/330px-Cytochrome_C_Oxidase_1OCC_in_Membrane_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Cytochrome_C_Oxidase_1OCC_in_Membrane_2.png/440px-Cytochrome_C_Oxidase_1OCC_in_Membrane_2.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cytochrome_C_Oxidase_1OCC_in_Membrane_2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A region of the <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_c_oxidase" title="Cytochrome c oxidase">cytochrome c oxidase</a> <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> is used to distinguish species in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barcode_of_Life_Data_Systems" title="Barcode of Life Data Systems">Barcode of Life Data Systems</a> database.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a>, genes can move freely even between distantly related bacteria, possibly extending to the whole bacterial domain. As a rule of thumb, microbiologists have assumed that members of <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a> or <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">Archaea</a> with <a href="/wiki/16S_ribosomal_RNA" title="16S ribosomal RNA">16S ribosomal RNA</a> gene sequences more similar than 97% to each other need to be checked by <a href="/wiki/DNA%E2%80%93DNA_hybridization" title="DNA–DNA hybridization">DNA–DNA hybridisation</a> to decide if they belong to the same species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> This concept was narrowed in 2006 to a similarity of 98.7%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Average_nucleotide_identity" title="Average nucleotide identity">average nucleotide identity</a> method quantifies <a href="/wiki/Genetic_distance" title="Genetic distance">genetic distance</a> between entire <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genomes</a>, using regions of about 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairs</a>. With enough data from genomes of one genus, algorithms can be used to categorize species, as for <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_avellanae" title="Pseudomonas avellanae">Pseudomonas avellanae</a></i> in 2013,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Land_2015_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Land_2015-22">[22]</a></sup> and for all sequenced bacteria and archaea since 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/DNA_barcoding" title="DNA barcoding">DNA barcoding</a> has been proposed as a way to distinguish species suitable even for non-specialists to use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> One of the barcodes is a region of mitochondrial DNA within the gene for <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_c_oxidase" title="Cytochrome c oxidase">cytochrome c oxidase</a>. A database, <a href="/wiki/Barcode_of_Life_Data_System" title="Barcode of Life Data System">Barcode of Life Data System</a>, contains DNA barcode sequences from over 190,000 species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ratnasingham_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ratnasingham-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stoeckle_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stoeckle-26">[26]</a></sup> However, scientists such as Rob DeSalle have expressed concern that classical taxonomy and DNA barcoding, which they consider a misnomer, need to be reconciled, as they delimit species differently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeSalleEgan2005_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeSalleEgan2005-27">[27]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_introgression" title="Genetic introgression">Genetic introgression</a> mediated by <a href="/wiki/Endosymbiont" title="Endosymbiont">endosymbionts</a> and other vectors can further make barcodes ineffective in the identification of species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Phylogenetic_species"></span><span class="anchor" id="Cladistic_species"></span><span class="anchor" id="Phylogenetic_species_concept"></span>
<span class="anchor" id="Phylogenetic,_cladistic,_or_evolutionary_species"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogenetic_or_cladistic_species">Phylogenetic or cladistic species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Phylogenetic or cladistic species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cladistic_species.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Cladistic_species.svg/170px-Cladistic_species.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Cladistic_species.svg/255px-Cladistic_species.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Cladistic_species.svg/340px-Cladistic_species.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cladistic_species.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cladistic or phylogenetic species concept is that a species is the smallest lineage which is distinguished by a unique set of either genetic or morphological traits. No claim is made about reproductive isolation, making the concept useful also in palaeontology where only fossil evidence is available.</div></div></div>
<p>A phylogenetic or <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">cladistic</a> species is "the smallest aggregation of populations (sexual) or lineages (asexual) diagnosable by a unique combination of character states in comparable individuals (semaphoronts)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nixon1990_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nixon1990-29">[29]</a></sup> The empirical basis – observed character states – provides the evidence to support hypotheses about evolutionarily divergent lineages that have maintained their hereditary integrity through time and space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Molecular markers may be used to determine diagnostic genetic differences in the nuclear or <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> of various species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giraud2008_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giraud2008-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nixon1990_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nixon1990-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2000_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2000-35">[35]</a></sup> For example, in a study done on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, studying the nucleotide characters using cladistic species produced the most accurate results in recognising the numerous fungi species of all the concepts studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2000_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2000-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Versions of the phylogenetic species concept that emphasise monophyly or diagnosability<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZachos201691–92_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZachos201691–92-37">[37]</a></sup> may lead to splitting of existing species, for example in <a href="/wiki/Bovidae" title="Bovidae">Bovidae</a>, by recognising old subspecies as species, despite the fact that there are no reproductive barriers, and populations may intergrade morphologically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Others have called this approach <a href="/wiki/Taxonomic_inflation" title="Taxonomic inflation">taxonomic inflation</a>, diluting the species concept and making taxonomy unstable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Yet others defend this approach, considering "taxonomic inflation" pejorative and labelling the opposing view as "taxonomic conservatism"; claiming it is politically expedient to split species and recognise smaller populations at the species level, because this means they can more easily be included as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endangered" title="Endangered">endangered</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUCN" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_list" title="Red list">red list</a> and can attract conservation legislation and funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike the biological species concept, a cladistic species does not rely on reproductive isolation – its criteria are independent of processes that are integral in other concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nixon1990_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nixon1990-29">[29]</a></sup> Therefore, it applies to asexual lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giraud2008_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Giraud2008-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2000_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2000-35">[35]</a></sup> However, it does not always provide clear cut and intuitively satisfying boundaries between taxa, and may require multiple sources of evidence, such as more than one polymorphic locus, to give plausible results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2000_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2000-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_species">Evolutionary species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Evolutionary species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An evolutionary species, suggested by <a href="/wiki/George_Gaylord_Simpson" title="George Gaylord Simpson">George Gaylord Simpson</a> in 1951, is "an entity composed of organisms which maintains its identity from other such entities through time and over space, and which has its own independent evolutionary fate and historical tendencies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Claridge1997_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Claridge1997-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> This differs from the biological species concept in embodying persistence over time. Wiley and Mayden stated that they see the evolutionary species concept as "identical" to <a href="/wiki/Willi_Hennig" title="Willi Hennig">Willi Hennig</a>'s species-as-lineages concept, and asserted that the biological species concept, "the several versions" of the phylogenetic species concept, and the idea that species are of the same kind as higher taxa are not suitable for biodiversity studies (with the intention of estimating the number of species accurately). They further suggested that the concept works for both asexual and sexually-reproducing species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> A version of the concept is Kevin de Queiroz's "General Lineage Concept of Species".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecological_species">Ecological species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ecological species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An ecological species is a set of organisms adapted to a particular set of resources, called a niche, in the environment. According to this concept, populations form the discrete phenetic clusters that we recognise as species because the ecological and evolutionary processes controlling how resources are divided up tend to produce those clusters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ridley_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-ridley-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_species">Genetic species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Genetic species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A genetic species as defined by Robert Baker and Robert Bradley is a set of genetically isolated interbreeding populations. This is similar to Mayr's Biological Species Concept, but stresses genetic rather than reproductive isolation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In the 21st century, a genetic species can be established by comparing DNA sequences, but other methods were available earlier, such as comparing <a href="/wiki/Karyotype" title="Karyotype">karyotypes</a> (sets of <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allozyme" title="Allozyme">allozymes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> variants).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BakerBradley2006_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-BakerBradley2006-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionarily_significant_unit">Evolutionarily significant unit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Evolutionarily significant unit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Evolutionarily_significant_unit" title="Evolutionarily significant unit">evolutionarily significant unit</a> (ESU) or "wildlife species"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> is a population of organisms considered distinct for purposes of conservation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronospecies">Chronospecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Chronospecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chronospecies.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1099" data-file-width="724" decoding="async" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Chronospecies.svg/170px-Chronospecies.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Chronospecies.svg/255px-Chronospecies.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Chronospecies.svg/340px-Chronospecies.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chronospecies.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Chronospecies" title="Chronospecies">chronospecies</a> is defined in a single lineage (solid line) whose <a href="/wiki/Comparative_anatomy" title="Comparative anatomy">morphology</a> changes with time. At some point, palaeontologists judge that enough change has occurred that two species (A and B), separated in time and anatomy, once existed.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chronospecies" title="Chronospecies">Chronospecies</a></div>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaeontology" title="Palaeontology">palaeontology</a>, with only <a href="/wiki/Comparative_anatomy" title="Comparative anatomy">comparative anatomy</a> (morphology) from <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a> as evidence, the concept of a <a href="/wiki/Chronospecies" title="Chronospecies">chronospecies</a> can be applied. During <a href="/wiki/Anagenesis" title="Anagenesis">anagenesis</a> (evolution, not necessarily involving branching), palaeontologists seek to identify a sequence of species, each one derived from the <a href="/wiki/Pseudoextinction" title="Pseudoextinction">phyletically extinct</a> one before through continuous, slow and more or less uniform change. In such a time sequence, palaeontologists assess how much change is required for a morphologically distinct form to be considered a different species from its ancestors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Viral_quasispecies">Viral quasispecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Viral quasispecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Viral_quasispecies" title="Viral quasispecies">Viral quasispecies</a></div>
<p>Viruses have enormous populations, are doubtfully living since they consist of little more than a string of DNA or RNA in a protein coat, and mutate rapidly. All of these factors make conventional species concepts largely inapplicable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> A viral <a href="/wiki/Quasispecies_model" title="Quasispecies model">quasispecies</a> is a group of genotypes related by similar mutations, competing within a highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutagenic" title="Mutagenic">mutagenic</a> environment, and hence governed by a <a href="/wiki/Mutation%E2%80%93selection_balance" title="Mutation–selection balance">mutation–selection balance</a>. It is predicted that a viral quasispecies at a low but <a href="/wiki/Neutral_theory_of_molecular_evolution" title="Neutral theory of molecular evolution">evolutionarily neutral</a> and highly connected (that is, flat) region in the <a href="/wiki/Fitness_landscape" title="Fitness landscape">fitness landscape</a> will outcompete a quasispecies located at a higher but narrower fitness peak in which the surrounding mutants are unfit, "the quasispecies effect" or the "survival of the flattest". There is no suggestion that a viral quasispecies resembles a traditional biological species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimwegen1999_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nimwegen1999-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WilkeWang2001_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-WilkeWang2001-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElenaAgudelo-Romero2008_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-ElenaAgudelo-Romero2008-56">[56]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_on_Taxonomy_of_Viruses" title="International Committee on Taxonomy of Viruses">International Committee on Taxonomy of Viruses</a> has since 1962 developed a universal taxonomic scheme for viruses; this has stabilised viral taxonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fauquet2005_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-fauquet2005-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gibbs2013_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-gibbs2013-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Mayr.27s_biological_species_concept"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mayr's_biological_species_concept">Mayr's biological species concept</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mayr's biological species concept">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ernst_Mayr_PLoS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1378" data-file-width="1964" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Ernst_Mayr_PLoS.jpg/220px-Ernst_Mayr_PLoS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Ernst_Mayr_PLoS.jpg/330px-Ernst_Mayr_PLoS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Ernst_Mayr_PLoS.jpg/440px-Ernst_Mayr_PLoS.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ernst_Mayr_PLoS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ernst_Mayr" title="Ernst Mayr">Ernst Mayr</a> proposed the widely used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_Species_Concept" title="Biological Species Concept">Biological Species Concept</a> of reproductive isolation in 1942.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Species_problem#Mayr's_Biological_Species_Concept" title="Species problem">Species problem § Mayr's Biological Species Concept</a></div>
<p>Most modern textbooks make use of <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Mayr" title="Ernst Mayr">Ernst Mayr</a>'s 1942 definition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_Species_Concept" title="Biological Species Concept">Biological Species Concept</a> as a basis for further discussion on the definition of species. It is also called a reproductive or isolation concept. This defines a species as<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Queiroz_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Queiroz-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>groups of actually or potentially interbreeding natural populations, which are reproductively isolated from other such groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Queiroz_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Queiroz-62">[62]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>It has been argued that this definition is a natural consequence of the effect of sexual reproduction on the dynamics of natural selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Mayr's use of the adjective "potentially" has been a point of debate; some interpretations exclude unusual or artificial matings that occur only in captivity, or that involve animals capable of mating but that do not normally do so in the wild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Queiroz_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-Queiroz-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_species_problem">The species problem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: The species problem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Species_concept" title="Species concept">Species concept</a></div>
<p>It is difficult to define a species in a way that applies to all organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanage2013_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-hanage2013-67">[67]</a></sup> The debate about species concepts is called the <a href="/wiki/Species_concept" title="Species concept">species problem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Queiroz_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-Queiroz-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The problem was recognised even in 1859, when Darwin wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">On the Origin of Species</a></i>:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>No one definition has satisfied all naturalists; yet every naturalist knows vaguely what he means when he speaks of a species. Generally the term includes the unknown element of a distinct act of creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span id="When_Mayr.27s_concept_breaks_down"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="When_Mayr's_concept_breaks_down">When Mayr's concept breaks down</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: When Mayr's concept breaks down">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inoceramus_cripsii_Creta_sup_Bergamo.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1186" data-file-width="1108" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Inoceramus_cripsii_Creta_sup_Bergamo.JPG/220px-Inoceramus_cripsii_Creta_sup_Bergamo.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Inoceramus_cripsii_Creta_sup_Bergamo.JPG/330px-Inoceramus_cripsii_Creta_sup_Bergamo.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Inoceramus_cripsii_Creta_sup_Bergamo.JPG/440px-Inoceramus_cripsii_Creta_sup_Bergamo.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inoceramus_cripsii_Creta_sup_Bergamo.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaeontologist" title="Palaeontologist">Palaeontologists</a> are limited to morphological evidence when deciding whether <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> life-forms like these <i><a href="/wiki/Inoceramus" title="Inoceramus">Inoceramus</a></i> bivalves formed a separate species.</div></div></div>
<p>A simple textbook definition, following Mayr's concept, works well for most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-celled_organism" title="Multi-celled organism">multi-celled organisms</a>, but breaks down in several situations:
</p>
<ul><li>When organisms <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">reproduce asexually</a>, as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single-celled_organism" title="Single-celled organism">single-celled organisms</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parthenogenetic" title="Parthenogenetic">parthenogenetic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Apomixis" title="Apomixis">apomictic</a> multi-celled organisms. <a href="/wiki/DNA_barcoding" title="DNA barcoding">DNA barcoding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetics</a> are commonly used in these cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reekie2005_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reekie2005-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The term <i>quasispecies</i> is sometimes used for rapidly mutating entities like viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></li>
<li>When scientists do not know whether two morphologically similar groups of organisms are capable of interbreeding; this is the case with all extinct life-forms in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaeontology" title="Palaeontology">palaeontology</a>, as breeding experiments are not possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></li>
<li>When <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridisation</a> permits substantial gene flow between species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZachos2016101_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZachos2016101-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Ring_species" title="Ring species">ring species</a>, when members of adjacent populations in a widely continuous distribution range interbreed successfully but members of more distant populations do not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZachos2016156–157_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZachos2016156–157-80">[80]</a></sup></li></ul>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Willow_Warbler_Phylloscopus_trochilus.jpg/225px-Willow_Warbler_Phylloscopus_trochilus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Willow_Warbler_Phylloscopus_trochilus.jpg/300px-Willow_Warbler_Phylloscopus_trochilus.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Common_Chiff-Chaff_-_Italy_S4E1681_(19081363189)_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="Chiffchaff" data-file-height="681" data-file-width="748" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Common_Chiff-Chaff_-_Italy_S4E1681_%2819081363189%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/150px-Common_Chiff-Chaff_-_Italy_S4E1681_%2819081363189%29_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Common_Chiff-Chaff_-_Italy_S4E1681_%2819081363189%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/225px-Common_Chiff-Chaff_-_Italy_S4E1681_%2819081363189%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Common_Chiff-Chaff_-_Italy_S4E1681_%2819081363189%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/300px-Common_Chiff-Chaff_-_Italy_S4E1681_%2819081363189%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Willow_warbler" title="Willow warbler">willow warbler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_chiffchaff" title="Common chiffchaff">chiffchaff</a> are almost identical in appearance but do not interbreed.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Species identification is made difficult by discordance between molecular and morphological investigations; these can be categorised as two types: (i) one morphology, multiple lineages (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">morphological convergence</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryptic_species" title="Cryptic species">cryptic species</a>) and (ii) one lineage, multiple morphologies (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Phenotypic_plasticity" title="Phenotypic plasticity">phenotypic plasticity</a>, multiple <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">life-cycle</a> stages).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">horizontal gene transfer</a> (HGT) makes it difficult to define a species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melcher_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melcher-82">[82]</a></sup> All species definitions assume that an organism acquires its genes from one or two parents very like the "daughter" organism, but that is not what happens in HGT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bapteste2005_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bapteste2005-83">[83]</a></sup> There is strong evidence of HGT between very dissimilar groups of <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a>, and at least occasionally between dissimilar groups of <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melcher_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Melcher-82">[82]</a></sup> including some <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williamson_2003_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williamson_2003-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The evolutionary biologist <a href="/wiki/James_Mallet" title="James Mallet">James Mallet</a> concludes that
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>there is no easy way to tell whether related geographic or temporal forms belong to the same or different species. Species gaps can be verified only locally and at a point of time. One is forced to admit that Darwin's insight is correct: any local reality or integrity of species is greatly reduced over large geographic ranges and time periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallet_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mallet-18">[18]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Wilkins writes that biologists such as the botanist <a href="/wiki/Brent_Mishler" title="Brent Mishler">Brent Mishler</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mishler_2022_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mishler_2022-85">[85]</a></sup> have argued that the species concept is not valid, and that "if we were being true to evolution and the consequent phylogenetic approach to taxa, we should replace it with a 'smallest clade' idea" (a phylogenetic species concept).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkins_2022_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkins_2022-86">[86]</a></sup> Wilkins states that he concurs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mishler_Wilkins_2018_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mishler_Wilkins_2018-87">[87]</a></sup> with this approach, while noting the difficulties it would cause to taxonomy. He cites the ichthyologist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Tate_Regan" title="Charles Tate Regan">Charles Tate Regan</a>'s early 20th century remark that "a species is whatever a suitably qualified biologist chooses to call a species".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkins_2022_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkins_2022-86">[86]</a></sup> Wilkins notes that the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Philip_Kitcher" title="Philip Kitcher">Philip Kitcher</a> called this the "cynical species concept",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> and arguing that far from being cynical, it usefully leads to an empirical taxonomy for any given group, based on taxonomists' experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkins_2022_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkins_2022-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aggregates_of_microspecies">Aggregates of microspecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Aggregates of microspecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">Species complex</a></div>
<p>The species concept is further weakened by the existence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microspecies" title="Microspecies">microspecies</a>, groups of organisms, including many plants, with very little genetic variability, usually forming <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">species aggregates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heywood_1962_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heywood_1962-89">[89]</a></sup> For example, the dandelion <i><a href="/wiki/Taraxacum_officinale" title="Taraxacum officinale">Taraxacum officinale</a></i> and the blackberry <i><a href="/wiki/Rubus_fruticosus" title="Rubus fruticosus">Rubus fruticosus</a></i> are aggregates with many microspecies—perhaps 400 in the case of the blackberry and over 200 in the dandelion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> complicated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hybridisation_(biology)" title="Hybridisation (biology)">hybridisation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apomixis" title="Apomixis">apomixis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polyploidy" title="Polyploidy">polyploidy</a>, making gene flow between populations difficult to determine, and their taxonomy debatable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jarvis_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jarvis-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wittzell1999_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wittzell1999-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dijk2003_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dijk2003-93">[93]</a></sup> Species complexes occur in insects such as <i><a href="/wiki/Heliconius" title="Heliconius">Heliconius</a></i> butterflies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallet_2007_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mallet_2007-94">[94]</a></sup> vertebrates such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypsiboas" title="Hypsiboas">Hypsiboas</a></i> treefrogs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> and fungi such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fly_agaric" title="Fly agaric">fly agaric</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-nolines">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ripe,_ripening,_and_green_blackberries.jpg" title="Blackberries belong to any of hundreds of microspecies of the Rubus fruticosus species aggregate."><img alt="Blackberries belong to any of hundreds of microspecies of the Rubus fruticosus species aggregate." data-file-height="2418" data-file-width="3394" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Ripe%2C_ripening%2C_and_green_blackberries.jpg/169px-Ripe%2C_ripening%2C_and_green_blackberries.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Ripe%2C_ripening%2C_and_green_blackberries.jpg/253px-Ripe%2C_ripening%2C_and_green_blackberries.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Ripe%2C_ripening%2C_and_green_blackberries.jpg/337px-Ripe%2C_ripening%2C_and_green_blackberries.jpg 2x" width="169"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Blackberries belong to any of hundreds of microspecies of the <i><a href="/wiki/Rubus_fruticosus" title="Rubus fruticosus">Rubus fruticosus</a></i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Species_aggregate" title="Species aggregate">species aggregate</a>.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heliconius_mimicry.png" title="The butterfly genus Heliconius contains many similar species."><img alt="The butterfly genus Heliconius contains many similar species." data-file-height="2107" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Heliconius_mimicry.png/120px-Heliconius_mimicry.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Heliconius_mimicry.png/179px-Heliconius_mimicry.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Heliconius_mimicry.png/239px-Heliconius_mimicry.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The butterfly genus <i><a href="/wiki/Heliconius" title="Heliconius">Heliconius</a></i> contains many similar species.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Systematics-of-treefrogs-of-the-Hypsiboas-calcaratus-and-Hypsiboas-fasciatus-species-complex-(Anura-ZooKeys-370-001-g009.jpg" title="The Hypsiboas calcaratus–fasciatus species complex contains at least six species of treefrog."><img alt="The Hypsiboas calcaratus–fasciatus species complex contains at least six species of treefrog." data-file-height="758" data-file-width="756" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Systematics-of-treefrogs-of-the-Hypsiboas-calcaratus-and-Hypsiboas-fasciatus-species-complex-%28Anura-ZooKeys-370-001-g009.jpg/120px-Systematics-of-treefrogs-of-the-Hypsiboas-calcaratus-and-Hypsiboas-fasciatus-species-complex-%28Anura-ZooKeys-370-001-g009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Systematics-of-treefrogs-of-the-Hypsiboas-calcaratus-and-Hypsiboas-fasciatus-species-complex-%28Anura-ZooKeys-370-001-g009.jpg/180px-Systematics-of-treefrogs-of-the-Hypsiboas-calcaratus-and-Hypsiboas-fasciatus-species-complex-%28Anura-ZooKeys-370-001-g009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Systematics-of-treefrogs-of-the-Hypsiboas-calcaratus-and-Hypsiboas-fasciatus-species-complex-%28Anura-ZooKeys-370-001-g009.jpg/239px-Systematics-of-treefrogs-of-the-Hypsiboas-calcaratus-and-Hypsiboas-fasciatus-species-complex-%28Anura-ZooKeys-370-001-g009.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybridisation">Hybridisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hybridisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">Hybrid (biology)</a></div>
<p>Natural <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridisation</a> presents a challenge to the concept of a reproductively isolated species, as fertile hybrids permit gene flow between two populations. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Carrion_crow" title="Carrion crow">carrion crow</a> <i>Corvus corone</i> and the <a href="/wiki/Hooded_crow" title="Hooded crow">hooded crow</a> <i>Corvus cornix</i> appear and are classified as separate species, yet they hybridise freely where their geographical ranges overlap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BerkeleyDefHybrid_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-BerkeleyDefHybrid-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-nolines">
<li class="gallerycaption">Hybridisation of carrion and hooded crows permits gene flow between 'species'</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">

</div></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ring_species">Ring species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Ring species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ring_species" title="Ring species">Ring species</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Ring_species" title="Ring species">ring species</a> is a connected series of neighbouring populations, each of which can sexually interbreed with adjacent related populations, but for which there exist at least two "end" populations in the series, which are too distantly related to interbreed, though there is a potential <a href="/wiki/Gene_flow" title="Gene flow">gene flow</a> between each "linked" population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZachos2016188_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZachos2016188-98">[98]</a></sup> Such non-breeding, though genetically connected, "end" populations may <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sympatric" title="Sympatric">co-exist in the same region</a> thus closing the ring. Ring species thus present a difficulty for any species concept that relies on reproductive isolation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> However, ring species are at best rare. Proposed examples include the <a href="/wiki/Herring_gull" title="Herring gull">herring gull</a>-<a href="/wiki/Lesser_black-backed_gull" title="Lesser black-backed gull">lesser black-backed gull</a> complex around the North pole, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ensatina_eschscholtzii" title="Ensatina eschscholtzii">Ensatina eschscholtzii</a></i> group of 19 populations of salamanders in America,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Greenish_warbler" title="Greenish warbler">greenish warbler</a> in Asia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> but many so-called ring species have turned out to be the result of misclassification leading to questions on whether there really are any ring species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liebers_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liebers-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-nolines">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ring_species_seagull.svg" title='Seven "species" of Larus gulls interbreed in a ring around the Arctic.'><img alt='Seven "species" of Larus gulls interbreed in a ring around the Arctic.' data-file-height="622" data-file-width="552" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Ring_species_seagull.svg/160px-Ring_species_seagull.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Ring_species_seagull.svg/240px-Ring_species_seagull.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Ring_species_seagull.svg/319px-Ring_species_seagull.svg.png 2x" width="160"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Seven "species" of <i><a href="/wiki/Larus" title="Larus">Larus</a></i> gulls interbreed in a ring around the Arctic.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PT05_ubt.jpeg" title="Opposite ends of the ring: a herring gull (Larus argentatus) (front) and a lesser black-backed gull (Larus fuscus) in Norway"><img alt="Opposite ends of the ring: a herring gull (Larus argentatus) (front) and a lesser black-backed gull (Larus fuscus) in Norway" data-file-height="558" data-file-width="717" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/PT05_ubt.jpeg/180px-PT05_ubt.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/PT05_ubt.jpeg/270px-PT05_ubt.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/PT05_ubt.jpeg/360px-PT05_ubt.jpeg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>

</p>

</p>
