<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_naming">Taxonomy and naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Taxonomy and naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mountain-lion-01623.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1336" data-file-width="1999" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Mountain-lion-01623.jpg/220px-Mountain-lion-01623.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Mountain-lion-01623.jpg/330px-Mountain-lion-01623.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Mountain-lion-01623.jpg/440px-Mountain-lion-01623.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mountain-lion-01623.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cougar, mountain lion, panther, or puma, among other common names: its scientific name is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puma_concolor" title="Puma concolor">Puma concolor</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_and_scientific_names">Common and scientific names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Common and scientific names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The commonly used names for kinds of organisms are often ambiguous: "cat" could mean the domestic cat, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Felis_catus" title="Felis catus">Felis catus</a></i>, or the cat family, <a href="/wiki/Felidae" title="Felidae">Felidae</a>. Another problem with common names is that they often vary from place to place, so that puma, cougar, catamount, panther, painter and mountain lion all mean <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puma_concolor" title="Puma concolor">Puma concolor</a></i> in various parts of America, while "panther" may also mean the <a href="/wiki/Jaguar" title="Jaguar">jaguar</a> (<i>Panthera onca</i>) of Latin America or the <a href="/wiki/Leopard" title="Leopard">leopard</a> (<i>Panthera pardus</i>) of Africa and Asia. In contrast, the scientific names of species are chosen to be unique and universal; they are <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">in two parts used together</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> as in <i>Puma</i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Specific_name_(zoology)" title="Specific name (zoology)">specific epithet</a> as in <i>concolor</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Species_description">Species description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Species description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lacerta_plica_holotype_-_ZooKeys-355-049-g005.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2197" data-file-width="1216" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Lacerta_plica_holotype_-_ZooKeys-355-049-g005.jpg/170px-Lacerta_plica_holotype_-_ZooKeys-355-049-g005.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Lacerta_plica_holotype_-_ZooKeys-355-049-g005.jpg/255px-Lacerta_plica_holotype_-_ZooKeys-355-049-g005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Lacerta_plica_holotype_-_ZooKeys-355-049-g005.jpg/340px-Lacerta_plica_holotype_-_ZooKeys-355-049-g005.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lacerta_plica_holotype_-_ZooKeys-355-049-g005.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Type_(biology)" title="Type (biology)">type specimen</a> (<a href="/wiki/Holotype" title="Holotype">holotype</a>) of <i><a href="/wiki/Plica_plica" title="Plica plica">Lacerta plica</a></i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a> in 1758</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">Species description</a></div>
<p>A species is given a <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> name when a <a href="/wiki/Type_(biology)" title="Type (biology)">type specimen</a> is described formally, in a publication that assigns it a unique scientific name. The description typically provides means for identifying the new species, differentiating it from other previously described and related or confusable species and provides a <a href="/wiki/Validly_published_name" title="Validly published name">validly published name</a> (in botany) or an <a href="/wiki/Available_name" title="Available name">available name</a> (in zoology) when the paper is accepted for publication. The type material is usually held in a permanent repository, often the research collection of a major museum or university, that allows independent verification and the means to compare specimens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICZNexampleabstract_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICZNexampleabstract-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitchcock_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hitchcock-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smithsonian_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smithsonian-110">[110]</a></sup> Describers of new species are asked to choose names that, in the words of the <a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Zoological_Nomenclature" title="International Code of Zoological Nomenclature">International Code of Zoological Nomenclature</a>, are "appropriate, compact, euphonious, memorable, and do not cause offence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICZNRec25C_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICZNRec25C-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abbreviations">Abbreviations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Abbreviations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Books and articles sometimes intentionally do not identify species fully, using the abbreviation "sp." in the singular or "spp." (standing for <i>species pluralis</i>, Latin for "multiple species") in the plural in place of the specific name or epithet (e.g. <i>Canis</i> sp.). This commonly occurs when authors are confident that some individuals belong to a particular genus but are not sure to which exact species they belong, as is common in <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winston_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-winston-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Authors may also use "spp." as a short way of saying that something applies to many species within a genus, but not to all. If scientists mean that something applies to all species within a genus, they use the genus name without the specific name or epithet. The names of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genera" title="Genera">genera</a> and species are usually printed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italics" title="Italics">italics</a>. However, abbreviations such as "sp." should not be italicised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winston_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-winston-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>When a species's identity is not clear, a specialist may use "cf." before the epithet to indicate that confirmation is required. The abbreviations "nr." (near) or "aff." (affine) may be used when the identity is unclear but when the species appears to be similar to the species mentioned after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winston_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-winston-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identification_codes">Identification codes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Identification codes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With the rise of online databases, codes have been devised to provide identifiers for species that are already defined, including:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Biotechnology_Information" title="National Center for Biotechnology Information">National Center for Biotechnology Information</a> (NCBI) employs a numeric 'taxid' or <i>Taxonomy identifier</i>, a "stable unique identifier", e.g., the taxid of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> is 9606.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kyoto_Encyclopedia_of_Genes_and_Genomes" title="Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes">Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes</a> (KEGG) employs a three- or four-letter code for a limited number of organisms; in this code, for example, <i>H. sapiens</i> is simply <i>hsa</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/UniProt" title="UniProt">UniProt</a> employs an "organism mnemonic" of not more than five alphanumeric characters, e.g., <i>HUMAN</i> for <i>H. sapiens</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Integrated_Taxonomic_Information_System" title="Integrated Taxonomic Information System">Integrated Taxonomic Information System</a> (ITIS) provides a unique number for each species. The LSID for <i>Homo sapiens</i> is <a class="external free" href="urn:lsid:catalogueoflife.org:taxon:4da6736d-d35f-11e6-9d3f-bc764e092680:col20170225" rel="nofollow">urn:lsid:catalogueoflife.org:taxon:4da6736d-d35f-11e6-9d3f-bc764e092680:col20170225</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lumping_and_splitting">Lumping and splitting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Lumping and splitting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lumpers_and_splitters" title="Lumpers and splitters">Lumpers and splitters</a></div>
<p>The naming of a particular species, including which genus (and higher taxa) it is placed in, is a <i>hypothesis</i> about the evolutionary relationships and distinguishability of that group of organisms. As further information comes to hand, the hypothesis may be corroborated or refuted. Sometimes, especially in the past when communication was more difficult, taxonomists working in isolation have given two distinct names to individual organisms later identified as the same species. When two species names are discovered to apply to the same species, the older species name is given <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Principle_of_Priority" title="Principle of Priority">priority</a> and usually retained, and the newer name considered as a junior synonym, a process called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synonymy_(taxonomy)" title="Synonymy (taxonomy)">synonymy</a></i>. Dividing a taxon into multiple, often new, taxa is called <i>splitting</i>. Taxonomists are often referred to as "lumpers" or "splitters" by their colleagues, depending on their personal approach to recognising differences or commonalities between organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winston_112-3"><a href="#cite_note-winston-112">[112]</a></sup> The circumscription of taxa, considered a taxonomic decision at the discretion of cognizant specialists, is not governed by the Codes of Zoological or Botanical Nomenclature.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Broad_and_narrow_senses">Broad and narrow senses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Broad and narrow senses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sensu" title="Sensu">Sensu</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nomenclature_codes" title="Nomenclature codes">nomenclatural codes</a> that guide the naming of species, including the <a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Zoological_Nomenclature" title="International Code of Zoological Nomenclature">ICZN</a> for animals and the <a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Nomenclature_for_algae,_fungi,_and_plants" title="International Code of Nomenclature for algae, fungi, and plants">ICN</a> for plants, do not make rules for defining the boundaries of the species. Research can change the boundaries, also known as circumscription, based on new evidence. Species may then need to be distinguished by the boundary definitions used, and in such cases the names may be qualified with <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensu_stricto" title="Sensu stricto">sensu stricto</a></i> ("in the narrow sense") to denote usage in the exact meaning given by an author such as the person who named the species, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonym" title="Antonym">antonym</a> <i>sensu lato</i> ("in the broad sense") denotes a wider usage, for instance including other subspecies. Other abbreviations such as "auct." ("author"), and qualifiers such as "non" ("not") may be used to further clarify the sense in which the specified authors delineated or described the species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winston_112-4"><a href="#cite_note-winston-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Reproductive_species"></span>
<span class="anchor" id="Isolation_species"></span>
<span class="anchor" id="Biological_Species_Concept"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Change">Change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Species are subject to change, whether by evolving into new species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barton-121">[121]</a></sup> exchanging genes with other species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaux_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaux-122">[122]</a></sup> merging with other species or by becoming extinct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZachos201677–96_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZachos201677–96-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Speciation">Speciation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Speciation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">Speciation</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> process by which biological populations evolve to become distinct or reproductively isolated as species is called <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> was the first to describe the role of <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> in speciation in his 1859 book <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Origin_of_Species" title="The Origin of Species">The Origin of Species</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Speciation depends on a measure of <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_isolation" title="Reproductive isolation">reproductive isolation</a>, a reduced gene flow. This occurs most easily in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allopatric" title="Allopatric">allopatric</a> speciation, where populations are separated geographically and can diverge gradually as mutations accumulate. Reproductive isolation is threatened by hybridisation, but this can be selected against once a pair of populations have incompatible <a href="/wiki/Allele" title="Allele">alleles</a> of the same gene, as described in the <a href="/wiki/Bateson%E2%80%93Dobzhansky%E2%80%93Muller_model" title="Bateson–Dobzhansky–Muller model">Bateson–Dobzhansky–Muller model</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barton-121">[121]</a></sup> A different mechanism, phyletic speciation, involves one lineage gradually changing over time into a new and distinct form, without increasing the number of resultant species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayr1982_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mayr1982-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exchange_of_genes_between_species">Exchange of genes between species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Exchange of genes between species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tree_Of_Life_(with_horizontal_gene_transfer).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2370" data-file-width="1782" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Tree_Of_Life_%28with_horizontal_gene_transfer%29.svg/220px-Tree_Of_Life_%28with_horizontal_gene_transfer%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Tree_Of_Life_%28with_horizontal_gene_transfer%29.svg/330px-Tree_Of_Life_%28with_horizontal_gene_transfer%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Tree_Of_Life_%28with_horizontal_gene_transfer%29.svg/440px-Tree_Of_Life_%28with_horizontal_gene_transfer%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tree_Of_Life_(with_horizontal_gene_transfer).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">Horizontal gene transfers</a> between widely separated species complicate the phylogeny of <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer" title="Horizontal gene transfer">Horizontal gene transfer</a></div>
<p>Horizontal gene transfer between organisms of different species, either through <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridisation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antigenic_shift" title="Antigenic shift">antigenic shift</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Reassortment" title="Reassortment">reassortment</a>, is sometimes an important source of genetic variation. Viruses can transfer genes between species. Bacteria can exchange plasmids with bacteria of other species, including some apparently distantly related ones in different phylogenetic <a href="/wiki/Three-domain_system" title="Three-domain system">domains</a>, making analysis of their relationships difficult, and weakening the concept of a bacterial species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melcher_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Melcher-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaux_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vaux-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Louis-Marie Bobay and Howard Ochman suggest, based on analysis of the genomes of many types of bacteria, that they can often be grouped "into communities that regularly swap genes", in much the same way that plants and animals can be grouped into reproductively isolated breeding populations. Bacteria may thus form species, analogous to Mayr's biological species concept, consisting of asexually reproducing populations that exchange genes by homologous recombination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venton2017_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Venton2017-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BobayOchman2017_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-BobayOchman2017-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extinction">Extinction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Extinction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">Extinction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">Extinction event</a></div>
<p>A species is extinct when the <a href="/wiki/Endling" title="Endling">last individual</a> of that species dies, but it may be <a href="/wiki/Functional_extinction" title="Functional extinction">functionally extinct</a> well before that moment. It is estimated that over 99 percent of all species that ever lived on Earth, some five billion species, are now extinct. Some of these were in <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">mass extinctions</a> such as those at the ends of the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician" title="Ordovician">Ordovician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> periods. Mass extinctions had a variety of causes including <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic activity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_change_(general_concept)" title="Climate change (general concept)">climate change</a>, and changes in oceanic and atmospheric chemistry, and they in turn had major effects on Earth's ecology, atmosphere, land surface and waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StearnsStearns2000_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-StearnsStearns2000-133">[133]</a></sup> Another form of extinction is through the assimilation of one species by another through hybridization. The resulting single species has been termed as a "<a href="/wiki/Compilospecies" title="Compilospecies">compilospecies</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZachos201682_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZachos201682-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practical_implications">Practical implications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Practical implications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Biologists and <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservationists</a> need to categorise and identify organisms in the course of their work. Difficulty assigning organisms reliably to a species constitutes a threat to the <a href="/wiki/External_validity" title="External validity">validity</a> of research results, for example making measurements of how abundant a species is in an <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> moot. Surveys using a phylogenetic species concept reported 48% more species and accordingly smaller populations and ranges than those using nonphylogenetic concepts; this was termed "taxonomic inflation",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> which could cause a false appearance of change to the number of endangered species and consequent political and practical difficulties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agapow2004_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agapow2004-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hey2001_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-hey2001-137">[137]</a></sup> Some observers claim that there is an inherent conflict between the desire to understand the processes of speciation and the need to identify and to categorise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hey2001_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-hey2001-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Conservation laws in many countries make special provisions to prevent species from going extinct. Hybridization zones between two species, one that is protected and one that is not, have sometimes led to conflicts between lawmakers, land owners and conservationists. One of the classic cases in North America is that of the protected <a href="/wiki/Northern_spotted_owl" title="Northern spotted owl">northern spotted owl</a> which hybridises with the unprotected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/California_spotted_owl" title="California spotted owl">California spotted owl</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Barred_owl" title="Barred owl">barred owl</a>; this has led to legal debates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> It has been argued that the species problem is created by the varied uses of the concept of species, and that the solution is to abandon it and all other taxonomic ranks, and use unranked monophyletic groups instead. It has been argued, too, that since species are not comparable, counting them is not a valid measure of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>; alternative measures of phylogenetic biodiversity have been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_forms">Classical forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Classical forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle's biology">Aristotle's biology</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle's biology">his biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> used the term γένος (génos) to mean a kind, such as a <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">bird</a> or <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, and εἶδος (eidos) to mean a specific <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">form</a> within a kind, such as (within the birds) the <a href="/wiki/Crane_(bird)" title="Crane (bird)">crane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crow</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Old_World_sparrow" title="Old World sparrow">sparrow</a>. These terms were translated into Latin as "genus" and "species", though they do not correspond to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linnean_taxonomy" title="Linnean taxonomy">Linnean terms</a> thus named; today the birds are a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Class_(taxonomy)" title="Class (taxonomy)">class</a>, the cranes are a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Family_(taxonomy)" title="Family (taxonomy)">family</a>, and the crows a <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a>. A kind was distinguished by its <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">attributes</a>; for instance, a bird has feathers, a beak, wings, a hard-shelled egg, and warm blood.  A form was distinguished by being shared by all its members, the young inheriting any variations they might have from their parents. Aristotle believed all kinds and forms to be distinct and unchanging. His approach remained influential until the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leroi88_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leroi88-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fixed_species">Fixed species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Fixed species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Ray_from_NPG.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2878" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/John_Ray_from_NPG.jpg/170px-John_Ray_from_NPG.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/John_Ray_from_NPG.jpg/255px-John_Ray_from_NPG.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/John_Ray_from_NPG.jpg/340px-John_Ray_from_NPG.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Ray_from_NPG.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Ray" title="John Ray">John Ray</a> believed that species breed true and do not change, even though variations exist.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_chain_of_being" title="Great chain of being">Great chain of being</a></div>
<p>When observers in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Modern" title="Early Modern">Early Modern</a> period began to develop systems of organization for living things, they placed each kind of animal or plant into a context. Many of these early delineation schemes would now be considered whimsical: schemes included consanguinity based on colour (all plants with yellow flowers) or behaviour (snakes, scorpions and certain biting ants). <a href="/wiki/John_Ray" title="John Ray">John Ray</a>, an English naturalist, was the first to attempt a biological definition of species in 1686, as follows:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>No surer criterion for determining species has occurred to me than the distinguishing features that perpetuate themselves in propagation from seed. Thus, no matter what variations occur in the individuals or the species, if they spring from the seed of one and the same plant, they are accidental variations and not such as to distinguish a species ... Animals likewise that differ specifically preserve their distinct species permanently; one species never springs from the seed of another nor vice versa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2342" data-file-width="1938" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9.jpg/170px-Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9.jpg/255px-Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9.jpg/340px-Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> created the binomial system for naming species.</div></div></div>
<p>In the 18th century, the Swedish scientist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> classified organisms according to shared physical characteristics, and not simply based upon differences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> He established the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchy</a> of classification based upon observable characteristics and intended to reflect natural relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> At the time, however, it was still widely believed that there was no organic connection between species, no matter how similar they appeared. This view was influenced by European scholarly and religious education, which held that the categories of life are dictated by God, forming an <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian</a> hierarchy, the <a href="/wiki/Great_chain_of_being#The_Great_Chain_in_natural_science" title="Great chain of being"><i>scala naturae</i> or great chain of being</a>. However, whether or not it was supposed to be fixed, the <i>scala</i> (a ladder) inherently implied the possibility of climbing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutability">Mutability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Species&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Mutability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In viewing evidence of hybridisation, Linnaeus recognised that species were not fixed and could change; he did not consider that new species could emerge and maintained a view of divinely fixed species that may alter through processes of hybridisation or acclimatisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> By the 19th century, naturalists understood that species could change form over time, and that the history of the planet provided enough time for major changes. <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste Lamarck">Jean-Baptiste Lamarck</a>, in his 1809 <i>Zoological Philosophy</i>, described the <a href="/wiki/Transmutation_of_species" title="Transmutation of species">transmutation of species</a>, proposing that a species could change over time, in a radical departure from Aristotelian thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1859, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Russel_Wallace" title="Alfred Russel Wallace">Alfred Russel Wallace</a> provided a compelling account of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> and the formation of new species. Darwin argued that it was populations that evolved, not individuals, by <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> from naturally occurring variation among individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> This required a new definition of species. Darwin concluded that species are what they appear to be: ideas, provisionally useful for naming groups of interacting individuals, writing:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I look at the term species as one arbitrarily given for the sake of convenience to a set of individuals closely resembling each other ... It does not essentially differ from the word variety, which is given to less distinct and more fluctuating forms. The term variety, again, in comparison with mere individual differences, is also applied arbitrarily, and for convenience sake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metaphysical_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-metaphysical-150">[150]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
